Khilal of the Fingers in Wudu: Sunnah or Wajib?
Explaining the sunnah of passing water between fingers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khilal of the fingers during wudu and explain the proof and its classification as sunnah.

Core Idea
The imperative to perform khilal is interpreted as sunnah in Hanafi fiqh, not wajib.

The practice of passing water between the fingers and toes (khilal) is a well-known act of wudu. The Hanafi school classifies it as a sunnah mu'akkadah based on the prophetic command. In this lesson, we examine the textual proof and the reasoning behind the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific act being discussed? What is the Hanafi classification?
The act is khilal (passing water between fingers and toes). It is sunnah mu'akkadah.
2
Locate the dalil
Which hadith is cited? What is its source?
Hadith from Ibn Abbas: 'When you perform wudu, perform khilal between the fingers of your hands and feet.' (Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah)
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the imperative form convey the ruling? Apply Hanafi usul.
The imperative 'fakhlil' indicates wujub in theory, but without a qarinah (contextual evidence) it is understood as recommendation (nadb).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Do other schools agree? What is their view?
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider it wajib based on the same hadith.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
What is the mufta bihi position and why?
The relied-upon position is sunnah mu'akkadah, as per zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۸۹)(۸) اور انگلیوں کا خلال کرنا ۔ وجه : عن ابن عباس ان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال اذا توضأت فخلل بين اصابع یدیک و رجلیک ۔(ترمذی شریف ، باب ماجاء فی تخلیل الاصابع ص ۱۲ نمبر ۳۹ وابن ماجہ شریف، باب تخلیل الاصابع ص ۶۴ نمبر ۴۴۶ ) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ انگلیوں کا خلال کرنا چاہئے ۔
Translation: (89) And khilal of the fingers. Evidence: From Ibn Abbas that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'When you perform wudu, then perform khilal between the fingers of your hands and feet.' (Tirmidhi, Bab ma ja'a fi takhlil al-asabi', no. 39; Ibn Majah, no. 446). In this hadith it is that khilal of the fingers should be performed.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the sunnah of khilal of the fingers. The author provides a hadith from Ibn Abbas where the Prophet explicitly commands: 'When you perform wudu, perform khilal between the fingers of your hands and feet.' This command indicates a recommended act (sunnah) in Hanafi fiqh, as the imperative is understood for recommendation unless there is evidence for obligation. The Hanafi school holds that khilal of the fingers is sunnah, not wajib.

Key Terms
تخليل الأصابع (takhlil al-asabi'): Passing water between the fingers and toes during wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, what should one do between the fingers during wudu?
A) Wash them separately
B) Perform khilal
C) Pass water over them without moving fingers
D) Leave them dry

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, khilal of the fingers is a confirmed sunnah, not an obligation.

The Sunnah of Washing Each Limb Three Times in Wudu
Explaining the sunnah of washing each limb thrice with evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling (hukm level) of washing each limb three times in wudu and explain the evidence from the hadith of Uthman.

Core Idea
Washing each wudu limb three times is a confirmed sunnah; once suffices for the fard.

This lesson examines the sunnah of washing each limb three times during wudu. The author presents the hadith of Uthman ibn Affan, which the Prophet endorsed as his own practice. Understanding the distinction between the obligatory (fard) and the recommended (sunnah) is essential for proper wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is washing each wudu limb three times. Determine the hukm level: is it fard, wajib, or sunnah?
The text clearly states this is sunnah, not obligatory.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from Bukhari and Abu Dawud describing Uthman's wudu, which the Prophet equated with his own.
The hadith is a dalil naqli (textual evidence) with zanni thubut and dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's statement 'Whoever performs wudu like my wudu' indicates his approval of washing each limb three times, establishing it as a sunnah practice.
The explicit repetition 'three times' (thalathan) in the hadith is the key indicator.
4
Note the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that washing once is fard (obligatory), while washing three times is a sunnah muakkadah (emphasized recommended act).
This is consistent with the majority of schools.
5
Recognize the virtue
The hadith also mentions that proper wudu followed by two rak'ahs of prayer leads to forgiveness of sins, highlighting the importance of following the sunnah.
This encourages the practice but does not affect the validity of wudu.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (٩٠) [۹] تین مرتبہ ہر اعضاء کا دھونا۔ وجه : اس حدیث میں ہے کہ ہر عضو کو تین تین مرتبہ دھوئے۔ ان حمران مولی عثمان اخبره انه رأى عثمان بن عفان دعا باناء ، فافرغ على كفيه ثلاث مرار فغسلهما ، ثم ادخل يمينه فى الاناء فمضمض و استنثر ثم غسل وجهه ثلاثا و يديه الى المرفقين ثلاث مرار ، ثم مسح برأسه ، ثم غسل رجليه ثلاث مرار الى الكعبين ، ثم قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : من توضأ نحو وضوئى هذا ثم صلى ركعتين لايحدث فيهما نفسه غفر له ما تقدم من ذنبه - ( بخاری شریف ، باب الوضوء ثلاثا ثلاثا ص ۳۲، نمبر ۱۵۹ وابوداود شریف، باب صفة وضوء النبي ، ص ۲٦ نمبر ۱۰۸)
Translation: (90) [9] Washing each limb three times. Evidence: In this hadith: that every limb is washed three times. Humran, the freed slave of Uthman, informed him that he saw Uthman ibn Affan call for water, poured over his hands three times and washed them, then entered his right hand into the vessel, rinsed his mouth and nose, then washed his face three times, and his arms up to the elbows three times, then wiped his head, then washed his feet three times up to the ankles, then said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever performs wudu like my wudu then prays two rak'ahs without speaking to himself, his previous sins will be forgiven.' (Bukhari, Bab al-wudu' thalathan thalathan, no. 159; Abu Dawud, Bab sifat wudu' al-nabi, no. 108)
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage covers the sunnah of washing each wudu limb three times. The author provides a detailed hadith of Uthman's wudu, which the Prophet described as his own wudu. The act of washing each limb thrice is a sunnah muakkadah based on the Prophet's consistent practice. The Hanafi school holds that while washing each part once is sufficient (fard), repeating three times is superior and sunnah. The hadith also highlights the virtue of proper wudu followed by two rak'ahs of prayer.

Key Terms
تثليث الغسل (tathlith al-ghusl): Washing each limb three times during wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many times did the Prophet wash each limb in his wudu according to the hadith of Uthman?
A) Once
B) Twice
C) Three times
D) Four times

Key Takeaway
Washing each wudu limb three times is a confirmed sunnah, not an obligation; once suffices for the fard of wudu.

Wiping the Entire Head: Sunnah of Completeness
Explaining the sunnah of wiping the entire head once

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wiping the head in wudu, distinguishing the fard (quarter) from the sunnah (entire head once), and apply the hadith proof.

Core Idea
Wiping the entire head is a confirmed sunnah, not a fard obligation.

In Hanafi wudu, the minimal obligation for the head is to wipe one quarter. However, the Prophet ﷺ demonstrated a more complete practice: wiping the entire head once. This lesson clarifies that distinction, showing what is legally required versus what is recommended, and grounds the sunnah in the hadith of Abdullah ibn Zaid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its two rulings
The passage treats 'wiping the head' as one act but divides it into a fard (quarter) and a sunnah (entire head once). Note both levels.
The fard is the minimum; the sunnah is the complete emulation.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from Bukhari and Abu Dawud describing the Prophet's wudu. Identify that this is a sahih hadith (zanni al-thubut).
The hadith is ahad, so it establishes a sunnah, not a qat'i obligation.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly describes the Prophet wiping his entire head. This demonstrates the sunnah practice; it does not prove wujub because the Hanafi school already regards quarter as fard.
The same action proves different rulings based on other evidence.
4
Note the ikhtilaf with Shafi'i school
The Shafi'is hold that wiping any part of the head is fard (based on the same hadith). The Hanafis interpret the hadith as showing sunnah, and they rely on other evidence for the quarter fard.
The practical difference: a Shafi'i would repeat wudu if no part was wiped; a Hanafi would not if a quarter was wiped.
5
Apply the distinction in practice
In wudu, ensure you wipe at least a quarter of the head to fulfill the fard. Then complete the sunnah by wiping the entire head once as the Prophet did.
The sunnah is a separate act; missing it does not invalidate wudu.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۹۱) [۱۰] ایک مرتبہ پورے سر کا مسح کرنا۔ تشریح : چوتھائی سر کا مسح کرنا فرض ہے اور ایک مرتبہ پورے سر کا مسح کرنا سنت ہے۔ وجه : فقال عبد الله بن زيد نعم . . ثم مسح رأسه بيده فأقبل بهما و ادبر بدأ بمقدم رأسه حتى ذهب بهما الى قفاه ثم ردهما الى المكان الذى بدأ منه ثم غسل رجليه ( بخاری شریف ، باب مسح الرأس كله، ص ۱۳۷، نمبر ۱۸۵ ابوداود شریف، باب صفة وضوء النبی ۲۸، نمبر ۱۱۸) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ پورے کا مسح کیا۔
Translation: (91) [10] Wiping the entire head once. Explanation: Wiping a quarter of the head is fard, and wiping the entire head once is sunnah. Evidence: So 'Abdullah ibn Zaid said: Yes... then he wiped his head with his hand, moving them forward and backward, starting from the front of his head until he reached his nape, then returned them to the starting point, then washed his feet. (Bukhari, Bab mash al-ra's kullihi, no. 185; Abu Dawud, Bab sifat wudu' al-nabi, no. 118). In this hadith he wiped the entire head.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage addresses the wiping of the head during wudu. The author distinguishes between the fard (obligatory) and the sunnah. The fard is to wipe at least one quarter of the head (a Hanafi specific ruling). The sunnah is to wipe the entire head once, as demonstrated by the Prophet in the hadith of 'Abdullah ibn Zaid, who described the Prophet's wudu: wiping the entire head in a single motion from front to back and back to front. This sunnah confirms the completeness of the act beyond the minimal obligation.

Key Terms
استيعاب الرأس بالمسح (isti'ab al-ra's bi al-mash): Wiping the entire head with wet hands during wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, what is the sunnah regarding wiping the head in wudu?
A) Wipe a quarter
B) Wipe the entire head three times
C) Wipe the entire head once
D) Wipe the front part only

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi wudu, wiping the entire head once is a confirmed sunnah; the obligatory minimum is a quarter.

Wiping the Ears in Wudu – Sunnah or Fard?
Explaining the sunnah of wiping ears with head water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wiping the ears during wudu and identify the preferred method based on prophetic practice.

Core Idea
Wiping ears is sunnah muakkadah – best done with the head's remaining water.

This lesson clarifies a common point of practice: wiping the ears during wudu. While many assume it is fard, the Hanafi school classifies it as sunnah muakkadah, supported by clear hadith evidence. We will examine the ruling, the two permissible methods, and the preferred one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is wiping both ears during wudu, and the Hanafi ruling is sunnah muakkadah (emphasized sunnah), not fard.
2
Locate the dalil
Two hadith are cited: (1) Ibn Abbas describes the Prophet wiping his head and ears, outer and inner. (2) Abu Umama reports the Prophet said 'The ears are from the head.' These are the primary proofs.
Both hadith are from Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's action and explicit statement indicate that wiping ears is linked to the head wipe, but not as a fard component. The Hanafi usul principle: the Prophet's action establishes sunnah, not wujub, unless there is an imperative.
The word 'مِن' in 'الأذنان من الرأس' shows inclusion but does not impose obligation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools consider the ears part of the head and thus required to be wiped, but the Hanafi school maintains it is sunnah only. The difference is nuanced: both agree the ears are wiped, but the legal classification differs.
Hanafi: sunnah; Shafi'i also holds it as sunnah (not fard).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that wiping ears is sunnah, and it is better to use the remaining water from the head wipe rather than fresh water.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa and daily practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۹۲) [۱۱] دونوں کانوں کا مسح کرنا، چاہے سر کے پانی سے ہو۔ تشریح : دونوں کانوں کا مسح کرنا سنت ہے، اب اس کی دو صورتیں ہیں ایک یہ کہ سر کے مسح سے باقی ماندہ پانی سے مسح کرے، اور دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ الگ سے پانی لیکر مسح کرے، بہتر یہ ہے کہ سر کے باقی ماندہ پانی سے مسح کرے۔ وجه : (۱) حدیث یہ ہے . عن ابن عباس : ان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مسح برأسه واذنيه ظاهرهما و باطنهما (ترمذی شریف ، باب مسح الاذنین ظاهرهما و باطنهما، ص ۱۱ نمبر ۳۶ وابوداود، باب صفة وضوء النبی ، ص ۱٦، نمبر ۱۲) (۲) عن ابي امامة صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : توضأ النبي فغسل وجهه ثلاثا و يديه ثلاثا ، و مسح برأسه ، و قال : الاذنان من الرأس . (ترمذى، باب ما جاء ان الاذنین من الرأس - ص ۱٦، نمبر ۳۷ ) اس حدیث سے ثابت ہوا کہ کان کے اوپر اور نیچے کے حصہ کا سر کے ساتھ مسح کرنا سنت ہے۔
Translation: (92) [11] Wiping both ears, even with the water of the head. Explanation: Wiping both ears is sunnah. There are two ways: either wipe with the remaining water from wiping the head, or take new water. It is better to use the remaining water from the head. Evidence: (1) The hadith: from Ibn Abbas: the Prophet (peace be upon him) wiped his head and his ears, their outer and inner parts. (Tirmidhi, Bab mash al-udhunayn dhahirahuma wa batinahuma, no. 36; Abu Dawud, no. 12) (2) From Abu Umama (may Allah be pleased with him) said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) performed wudu, washing his face three times and his hands three times, and wiped his head, and said: 'The ears are from the head.' (Tirmidhi, Bab ma ja'a anna al-udhunayn min al-ra's, no. 37). This hadith proves that wiping the upper and lower parts of the ears with the head is sunnah.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage explains the sunnah of wiping the ears during wudu. The author states it is sunnah and provides two methods: using leftover water from the head wipe or fresh water, with the former being better. Two hadith proofs are given: Ibn Abbas describes the Prophet wiping both the outer and inner parts of the ears, and Abu Umama reports the Prophet saying 'The ears are from the head,' indicating they are included in the same wiping act. The Hanafi ruling is that wiping the ears is sunnah, not fard, and it is best performed with the same water used for the head.

Key Terms
مسح الأذنين (mash al-udhunayn): Wiping both ears (outer and inner surfaces) during wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the preferred source of water for wiping the ears during wudu?
A) Fresh water
B) Remaining water from head
C) Water from rinsing mouth
D) Any water is acceptable

Key Takeaway
Wiping the ears is a sunnah muakkadah in Hanafi fiqh, best performed with the leftover water from the head wipe.

The Sunnah of Rubbing (Dalk) During Wudu
Hadith-based proof for dalk as sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two sunnah acts of dalk from the hadith and apply the Hanafi ruling that dalk is sunnah, not fard.

Core Idea
Rubbing (dalk) during wudu is sunnah, completing purification as the Prophet did.

This passage presents two hadiths from the Prophet ﷺ that describe his practice of rubbing specific body parts during wudu. The first hadith from Ibn Umar shows the Prophet rubbing his cheeks and passing his fingers through his beard, while the second from Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Hubli describes him rubbing between his toes with his little finger. These actions form the basis for the Hanafi ruling that dalk is a sunnah act—recommended but not obligatory. Understanding this helps us perfect our wudu without overburdening ourselves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts of dalk mentioned
Read the two hadiths and note which body parts are rubbed: the cheeks (from the first hadith) and between the toes (from the second hadith).
The first hadith also includes running fingers through the beard, but the focus is on rubbing the cheeks.
2
State the Hanafi ruling on dalk
According to the Hanafi school, dalk is sunnah (recommended), not fard or wajib. It is not obligatory for the validity of wudu.
The ruling is based on the Prophet's practice, which indicates desirability rather than obligation.
3
Analyze the first hadith (Ibn Majah)
The hadith states: 'The Prophet ﷺ when he performed wudu, rubbed his cheeks somewhat, then intertwined his beard with his fingers from underneath.' This shows deliberate rubbing, not mere splashing.
The word 'عرك' (araka) implies rubbing with some force.
4
Analyze the second hadith (Bayhaqi)
The hadith states: 'I saw the Prophet ﷺ rubbing with his little finger between the toes of his feet.' This specifies both the digit (little finger) and the location (between the toes).
This indicates thoroughness in ensuring water reaches between the toes.
5
Determine the legal implication
Since dalk is sunnah, omitting it does not invalidate wudu, but performing it earns reward and ensures more complete purification. The Hanafi school emphasizes this as a recommended practice.
If someone forgets or skips dalk, their wudu is still valid, but they lose the reward of this sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۹۳) [۱۳] بدن کا ملنا۔ وجه : (ا) عن ابن عمر قال كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم اذا توضأ عرك عارضیه بعض العرك ثم شبك لحيته بأصابعه من تحتها . (ابن ماجه شریف ، باب ماجاء فی تخلیل الحیه، ص ۶۳ نمبر ۴۳۲) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ حضور وضو میں گالوں کو رگڑا کرتے تھے۔ (۲) سمعت ابا عبد الرحمن الحبلى يقول رأيت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يدلك بخنصره ما بين اصابع رجلیہ۔ (سن بیہقی ، با کیفیت اتکلیل ، ج اول ص ۱۲۴ نمبر ۳۶۰) اس میں ہے کہ رگڑتے تھے ۔
Translation: (93) [13] Rubbing of the body. Evidence: (1) From Ibn Umar said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) when he performed wudu, rubbed his cheeks somewhat, then intertwined his beard with his fingers from underneath. (Ibn Majah, Bab ma ja'a fi takhlil al-lihyah, no. 432). In this hadith he used to rub the cheeks during wudu. (2) I heard Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Hubli say: I saw the Prophet (peace be upon him) rubbing with his little finger between the toes of his feet. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, with commentary, vol. 1, p. 124, no. 360). In this he used to rub.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage addresses the sunnah of dalk (rubbing) during wudu. The author provides two hadith: one from Ibn Umar describing the Prophet rubbing his cheeks and then passing fingers through his beard, and another from Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Hubli describing the Prophet rubbing between his toes with his little finger. These actions show that rubbing the body parts during wudu is a sunnah practice, enhancing the purification beyond the mere washing.

Key Terms
دلك (dalk): Rubbing the body parts (e.g., cheeks, toes) during wudu to ensure thorough cleansing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith in this lesson, which body part did the Prophet rub with his little finger during wudu?
A) Between toes
B) Between fingers
C) Cheeks
D) Beard

Key Takeaway
Dalk (rubbing) of the cheeks and between the toes during wudu is a confirmed sunnah, enhancing purification but not obligatory for validity.

5 min
Muwalah in Wudu: Succession is Sunnah, Not Wajib
Explaining the sunnah of washing limbs without delay

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on muwalah in wudu and distinguish between the consequences of deliberate and unavoidable delays.

Core Idea
Muwalah is sunnah, not wajib — delaying does not break wudu, but unnecessary delay is makruh.

In wudu, washing each limb immediately after the previous one is called muwalah. While some schools consider it obligatory, the Hanafi school views it as a recommended practice (sunnah). This lesson clarifies the ruling, exceptions, and the effect of delays based on the classical Hanafi text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Note that muwalah refers to washing limbs in quick succession without prolonged interruption. The text rules it as sunnah (recommended).
The ruling level is sunnah, not fard or wajib.
2
Understand the exceptions
If limbs dry due to extreme heat before completing wudu, there is no harm. This shows that unavoidable delays are excused.
Extreme heat is given as an example; other genuine excuses also apply.
3
Analyze deliberate delays
Deliberately delaying without a valid reason makes the action makruh tanzih (slightly disliked), but the wudu remains valid.
Makruh tanzih does not require repetition; it is better to avoid.
4
Determine the validity of wudu
Emphasize that even if one delays, wudu is valid and not invalidated. This distinguishes the Hanafi position from schools that require continuity for validity.
Validity depends on fulfilling the conditions and pillars of wudu, not on muwalah.
5
Compare with opposing view
The Shafi'i school considers muwalah wajib (obligatory). The practical difference: a Shafi'i would need to repeat wudu if a significant delay occurs, while a Hanafi would not.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate; each school has its own dalil.

Source Text

ترجمه : (۹۴)[۱۳] پے در پے دھونا۔ تشریح : ایک عضو کو دھونے کے بعد فور دوسرے عضو کو دھونا، اس کو پیدر پے دھونا کہتے ہیں۔ یہ سنت ہے۔ شدید گرمی کی وجہ سے اعضاء خشک ہو جائے تو کوئی حرج نہیں ہے لیکن خواہ مخواہ دیر کرنا مکروہ ہے اگر چہ وضو ہو جائے گا۔
Translation: (94)[13] Washing in succession. Explanation: After washing one limb, immediately washing the next limb; this is called 'pay dar pay' (continuous) washing. This is sunnah. If due to extreme heat the limbs dry, there is no harm. But deliberately delaying is makruh, although the wudu is valid.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of muwalah (continuous succession) in wudu. The author defines it as washing each limb immediately after the previous one without prolonged interruption. This is considered sunnah. However, if due to circumstances like extreme heat the limbs dry between washes, there is no blame. But intentionally delaying without excuse is makruh tanzih (disliked). Importantly, if one delays, the wudu is still valid even if it's makruh. This reflects the Hanafi view that muwalah is not a condition of validity but a sunnah.

Key Terms
الموالاة (al-muwalah): Washing limbs in immediate succession without prolonged interruption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It invalidates wudu
B) It is sunnah
C) It is makruh but wudu is valid
D) It is wajib to avoid delay

Key Takeaway
Muwalah is a sunnah of wudu; wudu remains valid even if interrupted, but deliberate delay without excuse is makruh.

The Seven Sunnahs of Wudu
Lists seven sunnah actions of wudu with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven sunnah actions of wudu and distinguish each from the corresponding fard action.

Core Idea
Seven sunnah actions enhance wudu; omission is valid but reward is less.

Wudu has obligatory (fard) and recommended (sunnah) components. This passage enumerates seven sunnah acts that complete the wudu and bring extra reward. Understanding them helps you maximize the spiritual benefit of your purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven sunnahs
Read through the passage and list each sunnah action: washing three times, wiping entire head, wiping ears, rubbing (dalk), continuity (walā'), intention (niyyah), and following order (tartīb).
2
Distinguish fard from sunnah for each
For example, washing once is fard, three times is sunnah; wiping a quarter of the head is fard, whole head is sunnah.
The commentary explicitly states which is fard and which is sunnah.
3
Note the proof for each
The passage provides hadith or Quranic evidence for each sunnah. Identify the key narrator or verse.
For instance, the hadith of Abdullah ibn Zaid for wiping the whole head.
4
Understand the ruling without them
If any sunnah is omitted, wudu is valid but reward is incomplete. The passage emphasizes this for niyyah and continuity.
Exception: delaying without excuse is makruh.

Chapter on the Sunnahs of Wudu: (90) Washing three times. (91) Wiping the entire head once. (92) Wiping the ears, even with the water of the head. (93) Rubbing the body. (94) Continuity (washing immediately). (95) Intention. (96) Following the order as stated by Allah in His Book. Commentary: For (91): It is fard to wipe a quarter of the head, and sunnah to wipe the entire head once. Proof: Hadith of Abdullah ibn Zaid that the Prophet wiped his entire head. For (92): Wiping the ears is sunnah; it can be done with leftover water from the head or new water. Proof: Hadith that the Prophet wiped his head and ears. For (93): Rubbing the body parts during wudu is sunnah. Proof: Hadith on rubbing cheeks and between fingers. For (94): Continuity means washing the next part immediately; it is sunnah. Delaying without excuse is makruh. For (95): Intention for wudu is sunnah; without it wudu is valid but reward is less. Proof: Hadith 'Actions are by intentions.' For (96): Following the order of limbs as in the Quran is sunnah. Proof: The verse of wudu and the practice of the Prophet.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a section from Thamaratun Najah listing the sunnah actions of wudu. The author enumerates seven sunnahs: washing each limb three times, wiping the entire head once, wiping the ears, rubbing the limbs (dalk), performing acts in immediate succession (walā'), making intention (niyyah), and following the prescribed order (tartīb). For each, the text provides a brief definition and supporting evidence from hadith or Quran. The commentary clarifies that these actions are recommended but not obligatory; they enhance the wudu and bring reward. The author distinguishes between fard (obligatory) and sunnah, e.g., wiping a quarter of the head is fard, while wiping the entire head is sunnah. Similarly, intention is not required for validity but for reward. The proofs cited are from authentic hadith collections such as Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud, and Tirmidhi. The passage also notes that continuity is sunnah, but if there is a valid excuse like extreme heat, delay is permissible. Overall, this section provides a concise guide for the student to understand and practice the sunnah aspects of wudu.

Key Terms
سنة (sunnah): In this passage, sunnah refers to recommended actions that are not obligatory; performing them earns reward, omission does not invalidate wudu.
مسح (masah): Wiping a wet hand over a body part; for wudu, wiping the head is fard (at least quarter), while wiping the entire head and ears are sunnah.
نية (niyyah): Intention to perform wudu; according to this text, it is sunnah for reward, not for validity.

The seven sunnahs of wudu enhance the act but are not required for validity.

Intention (Niyyah) in Wudu: Sunnah According to the Hanafi School
States in Urdu: intention in wudu is sunnah with two hadith proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intention in wudu, explain the two hadith proofs, and distinguish between the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions.

Core Idea
Intention in wudu is sunnah, not fard—reward depends on it, but validity does not.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position that making intention (niyyah) for wudu is sunnah, not obligatory. The text provides two hadith proofs and explains why intention affects reward but not the validity of wudu. This ruling distinguishes the Hanafi school from the Shafi'i school, which holds intention to be fard. Understanding this difference deepens your grasp of how proofs are weighed in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its level
The text states that intention in wudu is sunnah. Note the distinction between validity and reward: wudu without intention is still valid, but no reward is earned. This places intention in the category of sunnah muakkadah, not fard or wajib.
In Hanafi usul, sunnah muakkadah is a confirmed recommended act; omitting it is not sinful but misses reward.
2
Examine the first hadith (innama al-a'mal bi al-niyyat)
The hadith from Bukhari emphasizes that actions are judged by intentions. The Hanafi school interprets this as affecting the reward of actions, not their legal validity. Hence, wudu without intention is valid but unrewarded.
The same hadith is used by the Shafi'i school to argue that intention is fard, showing how the same text can yield different rulings based on usul principles.
3
Examine the second hadith (no wudu without mentioning Allah)
The hadith from Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud states that there is no wudu for one who does not mention Allah's name (tasmiyah). The author argues that this implies intention, as tasmiyah itself requires intention. Thus, intention is included by implication.
This hadith is ahad, so it does not impose obligation in Hanafi usul unless the command is explicit.
4
Understand the cross-madhhab dispute
The Shafi'i school considers intention fard for wudu, using the same hadith as a command. The Hanafi school responds that the hadith does not explicitly command intention, and ahad hadith without a clear imperative cannot create obligation.
This dispute highlights the impact of usul al-fiqh on fiqh rulings.
5
Formulate the Hanafi mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that intention is sunnah muakkadah. Wudu without intention is valid but lacks the reward of a sunnah act. Muftis should advise intending wudu for worship to gain full reward.
This position is based on the zahir al-riwayah from Imam Muhammad's core texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۹۵) (۱۴) نیت کرنا
تشریح : وضو کی نیت کرنا سنت ہے، اور نہیں کرے گا تب بھی وضو ہو جائے گا، البتہ ثواب نہیں ملے گا۔
وجه : عمر بن خطاب رضي الله عنه على المنبر يقول سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول انما الاعمال بالنيات وانما لكل امرئ ما نوى (بخاری شریف، باب کیف کان بدء الوحی الی رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ص ۲ نمبر ۱) اعمال کے ثواب کا دارو مدار یا اعمال کے صحیح ہونے کا دارومدار نیت پر ہے۔ بغیر نیت کے وضو کا ثواب نہیں ہوگا۔ اس لئے وضو میں وضو کی نیت کرنا سنت ہے۔ (۲) اس حدیث کے اشارے میں بھی ہے کہ نیت سنت ہے۔ ابی سفیان بن حويطب عن جدته عن ابيها قال سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول لا وضوء لمن لم يذكر اسم الله عليه (ترمذی شریف، باب فی التسمية عند الوضوء، ص ۱۳، نمبر ۲۵ ابو داؤد شریف، باب فی التسمیة علی الوضوء، ص ۱۸، نمبر ۱۰) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ بسم اللہ کے بغیر وضو ہی نہیں ہے، اس لئے اس میں نیت بھی آگئی۔
Translation: (95) (14) Making intention. Explanation: Making intention for wudu is sunnah; if one does not make intention, wudu will still be valid, but he will not receive reward. Proof: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) said on the minbar: I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say: "Actions are only by intentions, and every man has only what he intended." (Bukhari, chapter on how the revelation began, p. 2, no. 1) The reward of actions depends on intention, or the correctness of actions depends on intention. Without intention, there is no reward for wudu. Therefore, making intention in wudu is sunnah. (2) This hadith also indicates that intention is sunnah. Abu Sufyan ibn Huwaytib from his grandmother from her father said: I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say: "There is no wudu for the one who does not mention the name of Allah upon it." (Tirmidhi, chapter on tasmiyah at wudu, p. 13, no. 25; Abu Dawud, chapter on tasmiyah upon wudu, p. 18, no. 10) In this hadith it is that without saying Bismillah there is no wudu, so intention is included in it.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: intention (niyyah) in wudu is sunnah, not fard. The author begins by translating the ruling and explaining that wudu without intention is valid but unrewarded. The first proof is the famous hadith 'innama al-a'mal bi al-niyyat' from Bukhari, which the Hanafi school uses to argue that intentions affect reward, not validity. The second is a hadith about tasmiyah (saying Bismillah), which the author interprets as implying intention. The commentary follows the author's order, clarifying that intention is recommended but not required for validity.

Key Terms
نیت (niyyah): The deliberate intention to perform wudu for the purpose of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on intention (niyyah) in wudu?
A) Fard (obligatory)
B) Sunnah (recommended)
C) Wajib
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
Intention in wudu is sunnah in the Hanafi school; wudu without intention is valid but without reward.

Order of Washing Limbs in Wudu: Sunnah or Fard?
Explains that following the Quranic order of limbs in wudu is sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers the order of limbs in wudu as sunnah and identify the evidence from Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
The order of limbs in wudu is sunnah because the Quranic 'fa' indicates sequence but not obligation.

In wudu, we wash our face, hands, wipe our head, and wash our feet. But is this order a requirement or just a recommendation? The Hanafi school examines the Quranic verse and the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to determine the ruling. This lesson unpacks the evidence and reasoning behind the Hanafi position that the order (tartib) is sunnah, not fard.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Following the sequence of limbs mentioned in the Quranic verse when performing wudu. Determine the hukm (ruling) in the Hanafi school: is it fard, wajib, sunnah, etc.?
The ruling is sunnah muakkadah (emphasized sunnah), meaning highly recommended but not obligatory.
2
Locate the primary evidence (dalil)
What proof does the author cite? The Quranic verse from Surah al-Ma'idah (5:6) and the consistent practice of the Prophet in hadith. Identify the type of dalil (Quran, sunnah, etc.).
The verse is quoted directly: 'O you who believe! When you rise to prayer, wash your faces and your hands to the elbows, and wipe your heads and your feet to the ankles.'
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof leads to the ruling)
How exactly does this proof indicate that order is sunnah? Focus on the letter 'fa' at the beginning of each command (faghsilu, wamsahu, etc.). In Arabic, 'fa' often indicates sequence, but it does not by itself make the order obligatory. The command form (imperative) does not emphasize order; it simply lists the acts.
The Hanafi usul principle: al-amr yufidu al-wujub (the imperative indicates obligation), but the order itself is not explicitly commanded; only the acts are. The 'fa' is a particle of sequence, not obligation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (scholarly disagreement)
Which other schools disagree and why? The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools hold that order is fard (obligatory). Understand their evidence: they interpret the 'fa' as implying obligation and rely on hadith emphasizing the order.
The source does not detail the opposing evidence, but the difference is noted as a cross-madhhab dispute.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
What is the real-world difference? If a person performs wudu out of order, the Hanafi school says the wudu is valid but the sunnah is missed (makruh tanzihan to intentionally leave it). The Shafi'i school says the wudu is invalid if order is omitted intentionally or unintentionally.
This affects the validity of prayers performed with such wudu.

Translation: (96) (15) Performing in order according to the text of Allah's Book. Explanation: The order in which the limbs are mentioned in the verse is the order in which wudu should be performed; for example, the face is mentioned first, so wash the face first, then the hands, then wipe the head, and finally the feet are mentioned, so wash the feet last. Proof: (1) All hadiths on wudu mention washing the limbs in this order, which is present in the verse; therefore it is sunnah. The hadith has passed. (2) In this verse, the letter 'fa' at the beginning indicates order, so order in the remaining limbs is also sunnah. The wudu verse is: 'O you who believe! When you rise to prayer, wash your faces and your hands to the elbows, and wipe your heads and your feet to the ankles.' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:6)

This ruling states that following the sequence of limbs as in the Quranic verse is sunnah. The author uses the conjunction 'fa' in the verse to indicate sequential order. Hanafi scholars interpret this as a recommended order, not obligatory. The proof also cites that the Prophet consistently performed wudu in this order.

Key Terms

Start Wiping Head from the Front (مقدم الرأس)
Proves the sunnah of starting head wipe from the front with hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah method of wiping the head in the Hanafi school and identify the hadith evidence that supports starting from the front.

Core Idea
Start wiping the head from the front — this is the sunnah method, not mandatory.

In the Hanafi school, wiping the head during wudu is a fard act, but the manner includes sunnah aspects. This passage focuses on one sunnah: beginning the wipe from the front part of the head (muqaddam al-ra's). The evidence is a hadith describing the Prophet's wudu. Understanding this sunnah helps perfect wudu and follow prophetic practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act described
Recognise that the passage discusses the method of wiping the head (mas'h) during wudu.
2
Identify the specific recommendation
Note the instruction to start the wipe from the front of the head (muqaddam al-ra's).
This is a sunnah action, not a fard.
3
Locate the proof
Find the hadith from Muslim and Bukhari that describes the Prophet starting from the front.
The hadith explicitly states 'started from the front of his head'.
4
Determine the legal status
Classify the ruling as sunnah muakkadah (emphasised sunnah) based on the Hanafi position.
The evidence is a strong sunnah, not a requirement for validity.
5
Understand practical implication
Recognise that following this sunnah brings reward, but omitting it does not invalidate wudu.
The fard is to wipe the head; the starting point is a sunnah enhancement.

Wiping the Neck is Sunnah in Wudu
States ruling and proof for wiping neck

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on wiping the neck in wudu, identify the hadith proof, and distinguish wiping the neck from wiping the throat.

Core Idea
Wiping the neck is sunnah; wiping the throat is not.

In the Hanafi school, after wiping the head, it is a recommended sunnah to wipe the back of the neck with wet hands. This lesson presents the ruling and its evidence from a hadith, while clarifying that the throat is not included.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Locate the act being discussed: wiping the neck (masah al-raqabah). Note that the text says 'sunnah' and explicitly excludes the throat (halqum).
The hukm level is sunnah, not wajib or fard.
2
Examine the Proof
Read the hadith from Ibn Umar. The Prophet promises protection from chains (ghull) for those who wipe the neck during wudu.
The hadith is a single narration (khabar wahid) with zanni thubut.
3
Classify the Evidence
Identify the type of dalil: it is a Sunnah ahad hadith. Analyze its thubut (transmission) and dalala (indication).
Hanafi usul holds that ahad hadith can establish sunnah but not obligation.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Some scholars consider wiping the neck as bid'ah. The Hanafi school includes it as sunnah based on this hadith and other evidences.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; each position has its proofs.
5
Apply the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: a Hanafi who wipes the neck earns reward; one who omits it does not sin. A scholar from another school may not consider it part of sunan.
The difference is in reward, not validity of wudu.

The Hanafi school includes this as a recommended act.

The Sunnah of Starting with the Right Side and from the Fingertips in Wudu
First sunnah of wudu with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of starting with the right side and from the fingertips in wudu and explain the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Starting wudu with the right side and from the fingertips is a confirmed sunnah, not an obligation.

This lesson focuses on two specific sunnah acts of wudu: beginning each limb from the right side and washing the hands and feet starting from the fingertips. The author cites two authentic hadiths—one general from Aisha and one specific from the wudu of Uthman—to establish these practices as sunnah mu'akkadah. Understanding the proof helps distinguish sunnah from fard and deepens one's practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that among the sunnahs of wudu are 'al-bada'a bil-miyaman wa ru'us al-asabi''. The ruling is sunnah mu'akkadah (confirmed sunnah).
Sunnah mu'akkadah means the Prophet consistently performed it and recommended it.
2
Locate the dalil
Two hadith proofs are provided: (1) Aisha's report that the Prophet loved starting with the right in all good actions; (2) the report of Humran describing Uthman's wudu, which the Prophet said if done correctly leads to forgiveness.
Both hadiths are from Bukhari and Muslim, indicating high authenticity.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
From the first hadith, the phrase 'yu'jibuhu al-tayammun' (he liked starting with the right) shows preference, which in Hanafi usul implies recommendation (sunnah). From the second hadith, 'ila al-mirfaqayn' and 'ila al-ka'bayn' indicates starting from the fingertips as the natural beginning.
The proof is based on the Prophet's practice (fi'l) and his verbal endorsement.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
All schools agree it is recommended, but some (e.g., Shafi'i) consider it mustahabb while Hanafi classify it as sunnah mu'akkadah.
This difference affects the degree of emphasis but not the validity of wudu.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is based on the clear and consistent practice of the Prophet and his explicit love for starting with the right, which elevates it to sunnah mu'akkadah.
The mufta bihi is sunnah mu'akkadah; omitting it is makruh tanzihi but does not invalidate wudu.

فصل في سنن الوضوء
والبداءة بالميامن ورؤوس الأصابع (۹۸) ومقدم الرأس (۹۹) ومسح الرقبة لا الحلقوم
ترجمه : (۹۷) [۱۶] دائیں جانب اور انگلیوں کے سرے سے شروع کرنا۔ تشریح : ہر عضو کو دائیں جانب سے شروع کرے، اور دوسری سنت یہ ہے کہ مثلا ہاتھ کو اسکی انگلی کے سرے سے دھونا شروع کرے، اور پاؤں کو اس کی انگلیوں کے سرے سے دھونا شروع کرے۔
وجه : (۱) عن عائشة قالت : كان النبي عل الله يعجبه التيمن في تنعله ، و ترجله ، و طهوره ،وفی شانه کله 
۔ بخاری شریف ، باب التيمن في الوضوء والغسل ، ص ۲۹ نمبر ۱۲۸ر مسلم شریف ، باب التیمن فی الطھور، ج ۱۳۱ نمبر ۶۱۷/۲۶۸) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ آپ ہر اچھی چیز میں دائیں جانب کو پسند فرماتے تھے۔ اسلئے یہ سنت ہے ۔ (۲)۔ ان حمـــران مـولــی
عثمان اخبره انه رأى عثمان بن عفان دعا باناء ، فافرغ على كفيه ثلاث مرار فغسلهما ، ثم ادخل يمينه في الاناء فمضمض واستنثر ثم غسل وجهه ثلاثا و يديه الى المرفقين ثلاث مرار ، ثم مسح برأسه ،ثم غسل رجليه ثلاث مرار الى الكعبين ، ثم قال : قال رسول الله الله : من توضأ نحو وضوئى هذا ثم صلى ركعتين لا يحدث فيهما نفسه غفر له ما تقدم من ذنبه - ( بخاری شریف، باب الوضوء ثلاثا ثلاثا، ص۱۳۲، نمبر ۱۵۹ را بو داود شریف ، باب صفته وضوء النبی صلى الله عليه وسلم ص ۲۶ نمبر ۱۰۸) اس حدیث میں الی المرفقین ، اور الی الکعبین ، سے اشارہ ہے کہ انگلیوں کے سرے سے شروع کرتے تھے۔ لغت : ميامن : یمین سے مشتق ہے۔ دائیں جانب سے شروع کرنا۔ رؤس الاصابع : انگلیوں کا سرا۔
Chapter on the Sunnahs of Wudu. And starting with the right side and the tips of the fingers (98) and the front of the head (99) and wiping the neck but not the throat. Translation (97) [16]: Starting from the right side and the tips of the fingers. Explanation: He should start every limb from the right side, and the second sunnah is that, for example, he should start washing the hand from its fingertip, and the foot from its toes. Proof: (1) From Aisha who said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) liked to start with the right in putting on sandals, combing, purification, and in all his affairs. (Bukhari, Chapter on Tayammun in Wudu and Ghusl, p. 29 no. 128; Muslim, Chapter on Tayammun in Purification, vol. 1 p. 131 no. 617/268) This hadith indicates that he liked the right side in every good thing, so it is sunnah. (2) Humran, the freed slave of Uthman, informed him that he saw Uthman ibn Affan call for a vessel, poured water over his hands three times and washed them, then put his right hand into the vessel, rinsed his mouth and nose, washed his face three times, and his arms to the elbows three times, then wiped his head, then washed his feet three times to the ankles, then said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: Whoever performs wudu like this wudu of mine, then prays two rak'ahs without talking to himself, his previous sins will be forgiven. (Bukhari, Chapter on Wudu three times three, p. 132 no. 159; Abu Dawud, Chapter on Description of the Prophet's Wudu, p. 26 no. 108) In this hadith, 'to the elbows' and 'to the ankles' indicate that he started from the fingertips. Vocabulary: Miamin: derived from yamin, meaning starting from the right side. Ru'us al-asabi': fingertips.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil) for a sunnah of wudu. The author, Thamaratun Najah, states that among the sunnahs of wudu is starting with the right side and starting each limb from its fingertips. The proof is from two hadiths: the first from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) shows the general practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to begin with the right in all good actions, and the second from the wudu of Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) demonstrates that the Prophet started washing from the fingertips as indicated by the phrase 'to the elbows' and 'to the ankles'. The Hanafi school considers these acts as sunnah mu'akkadah, not fard or wajib, based on these authentic hadith. The language explains that 'miamin' means right side and 'ru'us al-asabi'' means fingertips.

Key Terms
ميامن (miyāman): Starting from the right side; plural of yamin.
رؤوس الأصابع (ru'ūs al-ʾaṣābiʿ): The tips of the fingers; the starting point for washing the hands and feet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fard (obligatory)
B) Wajib (necessary)
C) Sunnah mu'akkadah (confirmed sunnah)
D) Mubah (permissible)

Key Takeaway
Starting wudu with the right side and from the fingertips is a confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah) proven by authentic hadith and is highly recommended, though not obligatory.

Wiping the Neck in Wudu: Ruling and Evidence
Third sunnah of wudu with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wiping the neck in wudu, cite the hadith evidence, and explain the intra-madhhab difference in its legal status.

Core Idea
Wiping the neck is sunnah in wudu, but only the sides and back, not the throat – a practice rooted in hadith reward.

In addition to the obligatory acts of wudu, there are sunan (recommended acts) that perfect the ablution. This lesson examines the third sunnah: wiping the neck (al-raqabah). We will explore the textual proof from the Sunnah and the scholarly discussions within the Hanafi school regarding whether this act is a confirmed sunnah or merely mustahabb (recommended).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
The passage explicitly states that wiping the neck is sunnah, and wiping the throat is not. Determine what type of sunnah it is (mu'akkadah or ghayr mu'akkadah) based on the evidence strength.
The later comment mentions 'mustahabb', indicating a difference in emphasis.
2
Analyze the first hadith evidence
The hadith of Ibn Umar promises protection from shackles on the Day of Judgment for those who wipe their neck during wudu. Identify the chain and the import of the reward.
This hadith is a basheer (glad tiding) encouraging the act.
3
Analyze the second hadith evidence
The hadith of Talha's grandfather describes the Prophet wiping his head down to the nape of the neck. Note the description and its implication for the neck being part of the wiped area.
This hadith provides a practical precedent (fi'l).
4
Evaluate the intra-madhhab difference
The passage ends by noting that from these hadiths, some scholars consider it mustahabb rather than sunnah. Identify the basis for the difference and which position is relied upon in fatwa.
This reflects the later Hanafi discourse on weak evidence.
5
Contrast with other schools
Briefly note that the Shafi'i school does not consider neck wiping as sunnah, highlighting the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf.
The Hanafi position is distinct in including this act.

ومسح الرقبة لا الحلقوم (١٠٠)
ترجمه : (۹۹) [۱۸] گردن کا مسح کرنا سنت ہے،حلق کا نہیں ۔

حتى بلغ القذال وما يليه من مقدم العنق بمرة. قال : القذال : السالفة العنق (مسنداحمد ، باب حدیث جدطلحہ الایامی ، ج رابع ص ۵۳۱ نمبر (۱۵۵۲) ان احادیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ گردن کا مسح کرنا مستحب ہے۔
And wiping the neck but not the throat (100). Translation (99) [18]: Wiping the neck is sunnah, not the throat. Proof: (1) From Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: Whoever performs wudu and wipes his hands over his neck, he will be protected from the collar (shackles) on the Day of Judgment. (Al-Khisal al-Habir, Chapter on Sunnahs of Wudu, vol. 1 p. 34; Sharh Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din by al-Zabidi, vol. 2 p. 365; I'la' al-Sunan, Chapter on the Method of Wudu, vol. 1 p. 120) (2) From Layth from Talha from his father from his grandfather that he saw the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) wipe his head until he reached the back of the neck (qadhal) and what follows from the front of the neck once. He said: Al-qadhal is the nape of the neck. (Musnad Ahmad, Chapter on Hadith of grandfather of Talha al-Ayami, vol. 4 p. 531 no. 1552) From these hadiths, it is known that wiping the neck is mustahabb.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage discusses the third sunnah: wiping the neck (raqabah) but not the throat (halqum). The neck here means the side and back of the neck, not the front (throat). The proof includes a hadith from Ibn Umar promising protection from shackles on the Day of Judgment, and another from Talha's grandfather describing the Prophet wiping his head down to the back of the neck. Interestingly, the author notes that some scholars consider this mustahabb rather than sunnah, as seen in the concluding line. This reflects an intra-madhhab difference: the stronger view in the Hanafi school is that it is sunnah, but some later scholars considered it mustahabb. The key distinction is that the prophet is reported to have done it, but not consistently enough to make it a emphasized sunnah.

Key Terms
الرقبة (al-raqabah): The neck (back and sides), distinct from the throat (halqum).
الحلقوم (al-ḥalqūm): The throat, specifically the front part where the Adam's apple is.
القذال (al-qadhal): The back of the head/neck, the nape.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for wiping the neck in wudu according to the main Hanafi position in this passage?
A) It is fard
B) It is sunnah but not the throat
C) It is mustahabb
D) It is not prescribed

Key Takeaway
Wiping the neck (back and sides) is a sunnah act in wudu, supported by hadith, but its status varies from sunnah to mustahabb within the Hanafi school.

Status of Four Acts in Wudu: Sunnah or Mustahabb?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the status of four acts in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the four specific acts in wudu (tarteeb, right side, front of head, neck) are classified as sunnah mu'akkadah by the majority of Hanafis and identify the minority position that considers them mustahabb.

Core Idea
The four 'last acts' in wudu are sunnah mu'akkadah for the majority, but some classify them as mustahabb.

In this lesson we examine a gentle intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf about four specific acts in wudu. The majority view is that they are sunnah mu'akkadah, but some later scholars graded them as mustahabb. Understanding this difference refines your ability to apply the correct ruling and appreciate the nuance of Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts in dispute
Name the four acts: performing wudu in order (tarteeb), starting from the right side, beginning the head wipe from the front, and wiping the neck.
These are sometimes called the 'last four' sunan of wudu in the Hanafi school.
2
State the majority Hanafi position
The majority (including the position of Zahir al-Riwayah) holds that these acts are sunnah mu'akkadah based on hadith evidence.
Sunnah mu'akkadah means omission is disliked (makruh tanzihi).
3
Describe the minority view
Some later Hanafi scholars classify these acts as mustahabb (recommended but omission not disliked).
This is represented by the phrase 'Wa qeela' (and it is said).
4
Examine the thamarah al-khilaf
If sunnah mu'akkadah, omitting earns dislike; if mustahabb, omission is permissible without any dislike.
The practical difference is subtle but affects the grading of the act.
5
Note the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is sunnah mu'akkadah, as this is the view of the majority and of Zahir al-Riwayah.
In fatwa, the stronger view prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١٠٠) وقيل ان الاربعة الاخيرة مستحبة.
ترجمه : (۱۰۰) بعض حضرات کہتے ہیں کہ یہ اخیر کے چار مستحب ہیں۔ تشریح : بعض حضرات نے فرمایا کہ چار آخیر یعنی [1] ترتیب سے وضو کرنا [۲] دائیں جانب سے کرنا۔[۳] سر کے اگلے حصے سے مسح شروع کرنا۔ [۴] اور گردن کا مسح کرنا سنت نہیں مستحب ہیں ۔
(100) And it is said that the last four are mustahabb. Translation (100): Some people say that these last four are mustahabb. Explanation: Some people said that the last four, i.e., [1] performing wudu in order, [2] starting from the right side, [3] beginning the head wipe from the front, [4] and wiping the neck, are not sunnah but mustahabb.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the legal status of four specific acts in wudu. The author notes that some scholars (probably from later Hanafi authorities) consider these four acts—performing wudu in order (tarteeb), starting from the right side, beginning the head wipe from the front, and wiping the neck—as mustahabb (recommended but not established as sunnah mu'akkadah). This contrasts with the stronger position that they are sunnah mu'akkadah. The term 'al-arba'ah al-akhirah' refers to the last four of the sunan (or possibly the four mentioned in the preceding section). The practical consequence is that omitting these acts does not detract from the sunnah status; they are simply recommended. The Hanafi school generally maintains the sunnah position, but this ikhtilaf reflects the careful grading of acts.

Key Terms
مستحب (mustaḥabb): Recommended but not sunnah; an act whose performance is rewarded but omission is not disliked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Sunnah mu'akkadah
B) Mustahabb
C) Fard
D) Wajib

Key Takeaway
The four specific acts of wudu (tarteeb, right side, front of head, neck) are considered sunnah mu'akkadah by the majority Hanafi position, but a minority classifies them as mustahabb; the mufta bihi is sunnah mu'akkadah.

The Fourteen Mustahabb Acts of Wudu: Adab vs Sunnah
Defines the category of adab and lists fourteen mustahabb acts of wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fourteen mustahabb acts (adab) of wudu and explain why they are classified as mustahabb rather than sunnah.

Core Idea
The fourteen adab of wudu are mustahabb—reward for doing, no sin for leaving—distinct from sunnah.

Wudu is not only about obligatory and sunnah acts; there are also etiquettes (adab) that elevate the worship. This lesson clarifies the category of adab, lists the fourteen recommended acts, and explains the Hanafi position that these are mustahabb, not sunnah. We will also examine the evidence for one of them: seeking help in wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of ‘adab’
Read the passage’s essential note: adab are mustahabb—reward when done, no sin when omitted. They are below sunnah in emphasis.
Sunnah mu'akkadah omission is makruh; mustahabb omission is not.
2
Distinguish mustahabb from sunnah in Hanafi fiqh
The text explicitly says these are not sunnah but mustahabb. Some earlier scholars considered the last four as sunnah; the text sides with mustahabb.
The dispute about the last four is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
List the fourteen adab
Go through the list provided in the passage. Note that some are supported by hadith, others by general practice.
The text mentions hadith for some and refrains from repeating earlier ones.
4
Examine the proof for one adab: not seeking help
The hadith of Safwan ibn Assal shows the Prophet accepted help. The Hanafi interpretation: it is permissible when needed, but the adab is to do it independently.
The hadith is from Ibn Majah; it is ahad (zanni al-thubut).

(100) It is said that the last four are mustahabb. Chapter on the Etiquettes of Wudu. Essential note: The meaning of adab is that these things are not at the level of sunnah but at the level of mustahabb and adab; doing them increases reward, and not doing them incurs no harm. For this reason, for some adab, no hadiths are found, and for others, their hadiths have already passed, so they are not repeated for fear of length. The 14 etiquettes of wudu at a glance: The etiquettes of wudu are fourteen: ... Sitting in a high place, Facing the qiblah, ... Not seeking help from others (without need), Not speaking worldly things, Intending with both heart and tongue, Reciting the transmitted supplication, Reciting Bismillah and the transmitted supplication when washing each limb, Inserting the little finger into the two ear holes, Shaking the loose ring, Rinsing the mouth and snuffing water with the right hand, Cleaning the nose with the left hand, Performing wudu before the time if there is no excuse, Reciting the shahadah after wudu, Standing and drinking the leftover water of wudu and reciting this supplication: 'Allahumma aj'alni min al-tawwabeen wa aj'alni min al-mutatahhirin' i.e., O Allah, make me among those who repent and make me among the purified. ☆☆☆ Translation: (101) The fourteen etiquettes of wudu: (1) Sitting in a high place. Explanation: He sits in a high place so that the splashes of wudu do not fall on his body and clothes. Translation: (102) (2) Facing the qiblah. Explanation: This is a good deed, so one should face the qiblah while performing wudu. Translation: (103) (3) Do not seek help from others. Explanation: Because seeking help from others without need leads to laziness in worship and diminishes the dignity of the one performing wudu; therefore, seeking help in wudu is contrary to etiquette. However, if there is a need, one may seek help. The evidence for this is the hadith: 'From Safwan ibn Assal who said: I poured water for the Prophet (peace be upon him) in travel and stay for wudu.' (Ibn Majah, chapter on a man seeking help for his wudu, p. 58, no. 39). In this hadith, someone helped him in performing wudu. Translation: (104) (4) Do not speak worldly matters. Explanation: This is an act of worship, so it is etiquette not to engage in worldly talk during it. (105) (5) Combining the intention of the heart and the action of the tongue.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage from Thamaratun Najah defines the category of 'adab' (etiquettes) in wudu and distinguishes them from sunnah. The author begins by noting that some scholars consider the last four of the previously mentioned sunan to be mustahabb rather than sunnah—these are: performing wudu in order, starting from the right, beginning head wiping from the front, and wiping the neck. The chapter then lists fourteen etiquettes of wudu and emphasizes that 'adab' means these acts are at the level of mustahabb (recommended), not sunnah. Doing them brings extra reward; omitting them does not incur sin. Some of these adab have direct hadith evidence, while for others the author refrains from repeating hadiths already cited earlier. The fourteen adab are enumerated concisely. After the list, the text provides detailed commentary on the first five: (101) sitting in a high place to avoid water splashes, (102) facing the qiblah as a good practice, (103) not seeking help from others without need, with the permissibility in cases of necessity supported by the hadith of Safwan ibn Assal where he poured water for the Prophet ﷺ, (104) refraining from worldly talk because wudu is an act of worship, and (105) combining the intention of the heart with verbal utterance. The explanation follows the order of the matn and remains within the Hanafi understanding that these are mustahabb acts, not obligatory or sunnah mu'akkadah.

Key Terms
آداب (ādāb): Etiquettes or recommended practices that are mustahabb; they carry reward when performed but no sin for omission. They are distinct from sunnah (which is more emphasized) and wajib.
مستحب (mustahabb): A recommended act. Doing it earns reward, leaving it does not incur sin. Equivalent to mandub.
سنت (sunnah): A practice of the Prophet ﷺ that is stressed. In Hanafi fiqh, sunnah is a separate category between wajib and mustahabb; omission is disliked but not sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The fourteen adab of wudu are mustahabb, not sunnah, so performing them earns reward but omitting them incurs no sin.

The First Adab of Wudu: Sitting Elevated
First adab of wudu: sitting on elevated place

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the first recommended practice (adab) of wudu and explain its purpose.

Core Idea
Sit elevated during wudu to avoid impurity from splashes — a sunnah, not a fard.

This lesson introduces the first of fourteen recommended manners (adab) for wudu: sitting on a high place. While wudu itself is obligatory, these adab enhance its perfection and reflect the Prophet's practice. Understanding them deepens your appreciation of Islamic fiqh's attention to detail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
Recognize that the text describes a sunnah (recommended) practice, not a fard or wajib. The act is 'sitting on a high place' during wudu.
2
State the specific ruling
Confirm that sitting on a high place during wudu is mustahabb (recommended) in the Hanafi school.
Adab al-wudu are fourteen in total, and this is the first.
3
Explain the wisdom
The reason given is practical: to avoid water splashing onto one's body or clothes, which could carry impurity or cause inconvenience.
This reflects the fiqh principle of cleanliness and ease.
4
Distinguish from obligations
Note that adab are not obligatory. Omitting this practice does not invalidate wudu or require any compensation.
Compare with arkan (pillars) whose omission invalidates the act.

Facing the Qibla During Wudu: A Recommended Act
Second adab: facing qibla while performing wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on facing the qibla during wudu and identify its level as recommended (mustahabb) in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Facing the qibla during wudu is a recommended act, not an obligation.

This lesson covers the second adab (etiquette) of wudu: facing the qibla. While many acts of worship require facing the qibla, wudu is not one of them. However, it is a virtuous practice that brings reward. The classical Hanafi text Thamaratun Najah simply states this as a good deed, and we will unpack its legal status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that facing the qibla during wudu is an adab. Determine the legal classification (hukm) from the commentary: it is described as 'good practice' (achha kaam), indicating mustahabb (recommended).
The text does not explicitly say 'mustahabb', but the commentary implies it.
2
Understand the proof
No specific proof is cited here, but in Hanafi fiqh, this recommendation is based on general encouragement to face the qibla in acts of worship, derived from the Sunnah of the Prophet (peace be upon him).
Proofs for mustahabb acts often come from ahadith that praise the action.
3
Apply the ruling
Recognize that wudu is valid whether one faces the qibla or not. The recommendation does not affect validity; it only affects reward. This is a key distinction between wajib/fard and mustahabb.
If one faces away, wudu is still sahih.
4
Distinguish from other rulings
Contrast with salat, where facing the qibla is obligatory (fard). In wudu, it is merely a recommended adab. This shows that the same action can have different rulings in different contexts.

Adab 103: Not Seeking Help from Others in Wudu
Third adab: avoid seeking help from others during wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on seeking help during wudu and identify when it is permissible based on the Hanafi proof.

Core Idea
Seeking help in wudu without need is against adab; necessity allows it.

This lesson covers the third etiquette (adab) of wudu: not seeking assistance from others. The text explains that unnecessary help indicates laziness and pride, but if there is a genuine need, it is permitted. A hadith is cited as evidence that the Prophet himself received help during wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Recognize that the adab is about seeking help from others during wudu. The default ruling: it is makruh (disliked) and against etiquette.
2
Understand the rationale
The commentator gives two reasons: it shows laziness in worship and indicates a sense of superiority over the helper.
3
Note the exception
The text says: 'If there is a need, one may seek help.' This forms an exception to the default dislike.
4
Examine the dalil
The hadith from Safwan ibn 'Assal shows that the Prophet allowed others to pour water for him, indicating that receiving help is permissible when needed.
The hadith is ahad (single transmission) and zanni in both thubut and dalala.
5
Derive the ruling
Combine the default and exception: seeking help is makruh tanzihi without necessity, and mubah (permissible) with a valid excuse like illness or incapacity.

Source Text

He cites a hadith where Safwan ibn 'Assal poured water for the Prophet for wudu, indicating that receiving help is permissible when needed.

Adab 104: Not Speaking Worldly Talk During Wudu
Etiquette ruling for wudu: avoid worldly speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling regarding worldly speech during wudu and explain why it is considered etiquette.

Core Idea
Wudu is worship; avoid worldly talk to preserve its reverence.

This lesson covers the fourth adab of wudu: refraining from ordinary conversation. The commentator explains that since wudu is an act of 'ibadah, engaging in worldly talk goes against the spirit of devotion. Understanding this ruling helps maintain the proper adab without misunderstanding the validity of wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its status
Recognize that wudu is an act of worship ('ibadah). This is the basis for the etiquette ruling.
2
Recognize the ruling (hukm)
In Hanafi fiqh, speaking worldly talk during wudu is makruh tanzihi (disliked, not sinful). It is not haram.
3
Understand the rationale
Because wudu is worship, one should maintain focus and reverence. Worldly talk distracts from this.
4
Note what does NOT happen
Speaking does not invalidate wudu. The wudu remains sahih (valid), but the reward is diminished.
Contrast this with prayer, where speech invalidates.

Combining Heart and Tongue in Intention
Adab on synchronizing intention during wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the relationship between the heart's intention and the tongue's expression in wudu, and state the Hanafi ruling on the necessity of each.

Core Idea
The heart's intention is the essence; verbalizing it is recommended but not required.

This adab teaches us to align the internal resolve (niyyah) with its external utterance. In Hanafi fiqh, niyyah is a condition (shart) for wudu, located in the heart. The verbal pronouncement is a sunna that perfects the act and helps focus the mind.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components
Recognize that niyyah has an internal aspect (heart's resolve) and an external aspect (tongue's expression). The adab requires synchronizing both.
The heart's intention is obligatory; the tongue's expression is recommended.
2
Understand the Hanafi ruling
In Hanafi school, niyyah is a condition (shart) for the validity of wudu. It must be present in the heart at the start. Verbalizing it is mustahabb (recommended) and helps maintain focus.
The intention does not need to be uttered aloud; it is sufficient in the heart.
3
Apply the adab in practice
When performing wudu, one should both intend in the heart and, if convenient, verbalize the intention softly, ensuring the heart is attentive to the meaning.
Mere verbalization without heart's presence is insufficient.

Reciting Ma'thur Supplications During Wudu: The Sixth Adab
Sixth adab: recite prophetic supplications during wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on reciting ma'thur supplications during wudu and identify the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
Reciting prophetic supplications during wudu is a recommended adab, not an obligation.

In the Hanafi school, wudu is not merely a physical act but a spiritual one. The sixth recommended adab (etiquette) is to recite supplications that have been transmitted from the Prophet (ma'thur). This lesson examines the evidence from hadith and clarifies the ruling: it is mustahabb (recommended), not wajib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled? Reciting ma'thur supplication during wudu. The Hanafi ruling: mustahabb (sunnah).
The term 'adab' in the source indicates recommendation, not obligation.
2
Locate the dalil
Two hadith: one from Ibn Abbas showing the Prophet reciting a Quranic verse during wudu, another from Anas about reciting the shahada after wudu with promise of Paradise.
These are ahad hadith, providing zanni thubut and dalalah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's action (fi'l) indicates recommendation; the explicit reward in the second hadith encourages the practice.
Hanafi usul: fi'l of the Prophet is a proof of permissibility/ recommendation, not obligation.
4
Determine the thamarah
Omitting these supplications does not affect the validity of wudu; it is merely missing out on reward.
This distinguishes adab from shurut and arkan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالدُّعَاءِ. (۱۰۶) والدعاء بالماثورة.

ترجمه : (۱۰۶) [۶] ماثورہ دعاء پڑھنا۔
تشریح : وضو کرتے وقت حدیث میں جو دعا منقول ہے وہ پڑھنا ادب ہے۔

This establishes the recommendation of both Quran recitation and specific prophetic supplications.

Inserting the Pinky into Ear Holes During Masah
Ruling on inserting pinky into ear holes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of wiping the inside of the ears during wudu and cite the hadith proof from Abu Dawud.

Core Idea
Wiping the ears, including the inner part, is a recommended sunnah act, not an obligation.

This lesson covers the eighth adab of wudu: inserting the little finger (pinky) into the ear holes while wiping the head. Although some may think it is obligatory, the Hanafi school classifies it as mustahabb (recommended) based on the explicit practice of the Prophet as recorded in a hadith. Understanding this distinction helps avoid overburdening oneself and keeps worship within the bounds of the sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is inserting the little finger into both ear holes during masah of the head. According to the Hanafi school, this is mustahabb (sunnah ghayr muakkadah).
Mustahabb means recommended; omitting it does not invalidate wudu.
2
Locate the proof
The author cites a hadith from Amr ibn Shu'ayb narrated in Abu Dawud. In that hadith, the Prophet performed wudu and inserted his index fingers into his ears.
The hadith uses 'index fingers' (sibah) while the adab specifies 'pinky' (khunsur). This variation is acceptable as both are reported practices.
3
Analyze the dalil
The Prophet's explicit act of inserting fingers into the ears demonstrates that wiping the inside of the ears is part of the recommended practice. The warning against adding or subtracting shows its established nature.
The hadith is ahad and zanni in both thubut and dalala, hence the ruling is sunnah, not wajib.
4
Determine the thamarah al-amaliyyah
The practical effect is that if one omits wiping the inside of the ears, wudu remains valid, but the sunnah is missed. This distinguishes it from a fard or wajib act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۰۸) وادخال خنصره في صماخ اذنيه.

ترجمه : (۱۰۸) [۸] اپنی چھنگلی کو کان کے دونوں سوراخوں میں داخل کرنا۔
تشریح : سر پر سج کرتے وقت دونوں ہاتھ کی انگلیوں کو کان کے سوراخ میں داخل کرنا مستحب ہے۔
وجه : (۱) عن عمرو بن شعيب عن ابيه عن جده قال ان رجلا اتى النبي الله فقال يا رسول الله ! كيف الطهور ؟ فدعا بماء فى اناء فغسل كفيه ثلاثا ثم غسل وجهه ثلاثا ثم غسل ذراعيه ثلاثا ثم مسح برأ رأسه و ادخل اصبعيه السباحتين في اذنيه و مسح بابهامیه علی ظاهر اذنيه و بالسباحتين باطن اذنيه ثم غسل رجليه ثلاثا ثلاثا ثم قال هكذا الوضوء ، فمن زاد على هذا او نقص فقد اساء و ظلم او ظلم و أساء (ابوداود شریف)
(108) And inserting his pinky into the ear holes.

Translation: (108) [8] Inserting the little finger into both ear holes.
Explanation: During masah of the head, it is mustahabb to insert the fingers of both hands into the ear holes.
Proof: (1) From Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather that a man came to the Prophet and said: 'O Messenger of Allah, how is purification done?' So he called for water in a vessel, washed his hands three times, then his face three times, then his forearms three times, then wiped his head and inserted his two index fingers into his ears, and wiped with his thumbs on the outside of his ears and with the index fingers on the inside, then washed his feet three times each, then said: 'This is wudu; whoever adds to this or subtracts has done wrong and injustice.' (Abu Dawud)

Commentary
The eighth adab is inserting the little finger (pinky) into the ear holes during masah of the head. The commentator notes it is mustahabb (recommended). The hadith cited from Amr ibn Shu'ayb describes the Prophet's wudu, where he inserted his index fingers (not pinky) into his ears. This shows that wiping inside the ears is part of the sunnah, though the specific finger may vary in different narrations. The hadith also warns against adding or subtracting from this practice, indicating its established nature.

Key Terms
خنصر (khunsur): The little finger; used to wipe inside the ear holes during wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Wajib – omitting requires sajdah al-sahw
C) Sunnah ghayr muakkadah – recommended, omission does not affect validity
D) Mubah – optional, no reward or sin

Key Takeaway
Inserting the little finger into the ear holes during masah of the head is a sunnah act based on the Prophet's practice; it is recommended but not obligatory.
